      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg/220px-Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg/330px-Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg/440px-Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Charter_for_Collegiate_School_later_Yale_College_1701.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">Charter</a> creating Collegiate School, which became 
        <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a>, October 9, 1701
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><b>Yale University</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctoral_university" title="Doctoral university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/New_Haven,_Connecticut" title="New Haven, Connecticut">New Haven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>. Founded in 1701 in the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Connecticut" title="Colony of Connecticut" class="mw-redirect">Colony of Connecticut</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">university</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Colonial_Colleges" title="List of Colonial Colleges">third-oldest institution</a> of <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.</p> 
     <p>Originally chartered as the &quot;Collegiate School&quot;, the institution traces its roots to 17th-century clergymen who sought to establish a <a href="/wiki/College" title="College">college</a> to train <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> and political leaders for the colony. In 1718, the College was renamed &quot;Yale College&quot; to honor a gift from <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Yale" title="Elihu Yale">Elihu Yale</a>, a governor of the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company" class="mw-redirect">British East India Company</a>. In 1861, the Graduate School of Arts and Sciences became the first U.S. institution to award the <a href="/wiki/Ph.D." title="Ph.D." class="mw-redirect">Ph.D.</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a> in 1900. Yale College was transformed, beginning in the 1930s, through the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Residential_colleges_of_Yale_University" title="Residential colleges of Yale University">residential colleges</a>: <a href="/wiki/Yale_College#Residential_colleges" title="Yale College">12</a> now exist and two more are planned.</p> 
     <p>Yale employs over 1,100 faculty to teach and advise about 5,300 undergraduate and 6,100 graduate and professional <a href="/wiki/Student" title="Student">students</a>. Almost all tenured <a href="/wiki/Professor" title="Professor">professors</a> teach undergraduate courses, more than 2,000 of which are offered annually.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The University's assets include an <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">endowment</a> valued at $19.4&nbsp;billion as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> the second-largest of any academic institution in the world. Yale's system of more than two dozen libraries holds 12.5&nbsp;million volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">49 Nobel Laureates</a> have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_university_affiliation" title="List of Nobel laureates by university affiliation">affiliated</a> with the University as students, faculty, and staff. Yale has produced many notable <a href="/wiki/Alumni" title="Alumni" class="mw-redirect">alumni</a>, including five <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_education" title="List of Presidents of the United States by education">U.S. Presidents</a>, 19 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Justices_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="List of Justices of the Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court Justices</a>, and several foreign heads of state. Its law school is the most selective in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale students compete intercollegiately as the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bulldogs" title="Yale Bulldogs">Yale Bulldogs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/NCAA" title="NCAA" class="mw-redirect">NCAA</a> <a href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">Division I</a> <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a>. The oldest intercollegiate athletic event in the United States is the <a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Yale_Regatta" title="Harvard?Yale Regatta">Yale-Harvard regatta</a>.</p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg/170px-A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg/255px-A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg/340px-A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:A_Front_View_of_Yale_College_and_the_College_Chapel_New_Haven_printed_by_Daniel_Bowen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <i>A Front View of Yale-College and the College Chapel</i>, Daniel Bowen, 1786.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_History">Early History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early History">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Yale traces its beginnings to &quot;An Act for Liberty to Erect a Collegiate School&quot;, passed by the General Court of the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Colony" title="Connecticut Colony">Colony of Connecticut</a> on October 9, 1701, in an effort to create an institution to train ministers and lay leadership for Connecticut. Soon thereafter, a group of ten <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a> ministers: <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Andrew" title="Samuel Andrew">Samuel Andrew</a>, Thomas Buckingham, Israel Chauncy, Samuel Mather, <a href="/wiki/James_Noyes" title="James Noyes">James Noyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Pierpont_(Yale_founder)" title="James Pierpont (Yale founder)">James Pierpont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pierson" title="Abraham Pierson">Abraham Pierson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noadiah_Russell_(Yale_founder)" title="Noadiah Russell (Yale founder)" class="mw-redirect">Noadiah Russell</a>, Joseph Webb and <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Woodbridge" title="Timothy Woodbridge">Timothy Woodbridge</a>, all of whom were alumni of <a href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard" class="mw-redirect">Harvard</a>, met in the study of Reverend <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Russell_(Yale_co-founder)" title="Samuel Russell (Yale co-founder)">Samuel Russell</a> in <a href="/wiki/Branford,_Connecticut" title="Branford, Connecticut">Branford, Connecticut</a>, to pool their books to form the school's first library.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The group, led by <a href="/wiki/James_Pierpont_(Yale_founder)" title="James Pierpont (Yale founder)">James Pierpont</a>, is now known as &quot;The Founders&quot;.</p> 
     <p>Originally called the &quot;Collegiate School&quot;, the institution opened in the home of its first <a href="/wiki/Rector_(academia)" title="Rector (academia)">rector</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pierson" title="Abraham Pierson">Abraham Pierson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Killingworth,_Connecticut" title="Killingworth, Connecticut">Killingworth</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Clinton,_Connecticut" title="Clinton, Connecticut">Clinton</a>). The school moved to <a href="/wiki/Old_Saybrook,_Connecticut" title="Old Saybrook, Connecticut">Saybrook</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Wethersfield,_Connecticut" title="Wethersfield, Connecticut">Wethersfield</a>. In 1718 the college moved to <a href="/wiki/New_Haven,_Connecticut" title="New Haven, Connecticut">New Haven, Connecticut</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg/220px-Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg/330px-Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg/440px-Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_College_diploma_Nathaniel_Chauncey_1702.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> First diploma awarded by 
        <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a>, granted to Nathaniel Chauncey, 1702.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Meanwhile, a rift was forming at Harvard between its sixth president <a href="/wiki/Increase_Mather" title="Increase Mather">Increase Mather</a> and the rest of the Harvard clergy, whom Mather viewed as increasingly liberal, ecclesiastically lax, and overly broad in <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">Church polity</a>. The feud caused the Mathers to champion the success of the Collegiate School in the hope that it would maintain the <a href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> religious orthodoxy in a way that Harvard had not.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1718, at the behest of either <a href="/wiki/Rector_(academia)" title="Rector (academia)">Rector</a> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Andrew" title="Samuel Andrew">Samuel Andrew</a> or the colony's Governor <a href="/wiki/Saltonstall_family" title="Saltonstall family">Gurdon Saltonstall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Mather" title="Cotton Mather">Cotton Mather</a> contacted a successful businessman in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> named <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Yale" title="Elihu Yale">Elihu Yale</a> to ask him for financial help in constructing a new building for the college. Through the persuasion of <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Dummer" title="Jeremiah Dummer">Jeremiah Dummer</a>, Yale, who had made a fortune through trade while living in <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a> as a representative of the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company" class="mw-redirect">East India Company</a>, donated nine bales of goods, which were sold for more than &pound;560, a substantial sum at the time. Yale also donated 417&nbsp;books and a portrait of <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">King George&nbsp;I</a>. Cotton Mather suggested that the school change its name to <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a> in gratitude to its benefactor, and to increase the chances that he would give the college another large donation or bequest. Elihu Yale was away in India when the news of the school's name change reached his home in <a href="/wiki/Wrexham" title="Wrexham">Wrexham</a>, Wales, a trip from which he never returned. While he did ultimately leave his fortunes to the &quot;Collegiate School within His Majesties Colony of Connecticot&quot;,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the institution was never able to successfully lay claim to it.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg/220px-A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg/330px-A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg/440px-A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:A_View_of_the_Buildings_of_Yale_College_at_New_Haven_1807.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Old Brick Row in 1807.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Curriculum">Curriculum</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Curriculum">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Yale was swept up by the great intellectual movements of the period?the <a href="/wiki/Great_Awakening" title="Great Awakening">Great Awakening</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>?thanks to the religious and scientific interests of presidents <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clap" title="Thomas Clap">Thomas Clap</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>. They were both instrumental in developing the scientific curriculum at Yale, while dealing with wars, student tumults, graffiti, &quot;irrelevance&quot; of curricula, desperate need for endowment, and fights with the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_General_Assembly" title="Connecticut General Assembly">Connecticut legislature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Serious American students of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and divinity, particularly in New England, regarded <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a>, along with Greek and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and essential for study of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> in the original words. The Reverend <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>, president of the College from 1778 to 1795, brought with him his interest in the Hebrew language as a vehicle for studying ancient <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical texts</a> in their original language (as was common in other schools), requiring all <a href="/wiki/Freshmen" title="Freshmen" class="mw-redirect">freshmen</a> to study Hebrew (in contrast to Harvard, where only upperclassmen were required to study the language) and is responsible for the Hebrew phrase ????? ????? (<a href="/wiki/Urim_and_Thummim" title="Urim and Thummim">Urim and Thummim</a>) on the Yale seal. Stiles' greatest challenge occurred in July 1779 when hostile British forces occupied New Haven and threatened to raze the College. Fortunately, Yale graduate <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Fanning_(colonial_administrator)" title="Edmund Fanning (colonial administrator)">Edmund Fanning</a>, Secretary to the British General in command of the occupation, interceded and the College was saved. Fanning later was granted an honorary degree <a href="/wiki/LL.D." title="LL.D." class="mw-redirect">LL.D.</a>, at 1803,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> for his efforts.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg/220px-Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg/330px-Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg/440px-Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Woolsey_Hall_Memorial_Hall_University_Dining_Hall_Yale_University.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/Woolsey_Hall" title="Woolsey Hall">Woolsey Hall</a></i> in c. 1905
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Students">Students</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Students">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>As the only college in Connecticut, Yale educated the sons of the elite.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Offenses for which students were punished included cardplaying, tavern-going, destruction of college property, and acts of disobedience to college authorities. During the period, Harvard was distinctive for the stability and maturity of its tutor corps, while Yale had youth and zeal on its side.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The emphasis on classics gave rise to a number of private student societies, open only by invitation, which arose primarily as forums for discussions of modern scholarship, literature and politics. The first such organizations were debating societies: <a href="/wiki/Crotonia" title="Crotonia">Crotonia</a> in 1738, <a href="/wiki/Linonia" title="Linonia" class="mw-redirect">Linonia</a> in 1753, and <a href="/wiki/Brothers_in_Unity" title="Brothers in Unity">Brothers in Unity</a> in 1768.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg/220px-Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg/330px-Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg/440px-Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_Fence_men_sitting_and_leaning_on_it_facing_Chapel_Street_New_Haven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Men leaning on the old Yale fence facing Chapel Street, c.&nbsp;1874.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Yale Report of 1828 was a dogmatic defense of the Latin and Greek curriculum against critics who wanted more courses in modern languages, mathematics, and science. Unlike higher education in Europe, there was no national curriculum for colleges and universities in the United States. In the competition for students and financial support, college leaders strove to keep current with demands for innovation. At the same time, they realized that a significant portion of their students and prospective students demanded a classical background. The Yale report meant the classics would not be abandoned. At the same time, all institutions experimented with changes in the curriculum, often resulting in a dual track. In the decentralized environment of higher education in the United States, balancing change with tradition was a common challenge because no one could afford to be completely modern or completely classical.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time a group of professors at Yale and New Haven Congregationalist ministers articulated a conservative response to the changes brought about by the Victorian culture. They concentrated on developing a whole man possessed of religious values sufficiently strong to resist temptations from within, yet flexible enough to adjust to the 'isms' (professionalism, materialism, individualism, and consumerism) tempting him from without.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Perhaps the most well-remembered<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> teacher was <a href="/wiki/William_Graham_Sumner" title="William Graham Sumner">William Graham Sumner</a>, professor from 1872 to 1909. He taught in the emerging disciplines of economics and sociology to overflowing classrooms. He bested President <a href="/wiki/Noah_Porter" title="Noah Porter">Noah Porter</a>, who disliked social science and wanted Yale to lock into its traditions of classical education, 1879?81. Porter objected to Sumner's use of a textbook by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Spencer" title="Herbert Spencer">Herbert Spencer</a> that espoused agnostic materialism because it might intellectually, morally, and religiously harm students.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_and_debate">Sports and debate</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sports and debate">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The Revolutionary War hero <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Hale" title="Nathan Hale">Nathan Hale</a> (Yale 1773) was the prototype of the Yale ideal in the early 19th&nbsp;century: a manly yet aristocratic scholar, equally well-versed in knowledge and sports, and a patriot who regretted he had but one life to lose for his country. Western painter <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Remington" title="Frederic Remington">Frederic Remington</a> (Yale 1900) was an artist whose heroes gloried in combat and tests of strength in the Wild West. The fictional, turn-of-the-20th-century Yale man Frank Merriwell embodied the heroic ideal without racial prejudice, and his fictional successor Frank Stover in the novel <i>Stover at Yale</i> (1911) questioned the business mentality that had become prevalent at the school. Increasingly the students turned to athletic stars as their heroes, especially since winning the big game became the goal of the student body, and the alumni, as well as the team itself.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Along with Harvard and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton</a>, Yale students rejected elite British concepts about 'amateurism' in sports and constructed athletic programs that were uniquely American, such as football.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Yale_football_rivalry" title="Harvard?Yale football rivalry">Harvard?Yale football rivalry</a> began in 1875.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg/170px-Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg/255px-Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg/340px-Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yale%27s_four-oared_crew_team_with_1876_Centennial_Regatta_trophy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Yale's four-oared 
        <a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">crew</a> team, posing with 1876 Centennial 
        <a href="/wiki/Regatta" title="Regatta">Regatta</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Trophy" title="Trophy">trophy</a>, won at 
        <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Between 1892, when Harvard and Yale met in the first intercollegiate debate, and 1909, the year of the first Triangular Debate of Harvard, Yale, and Princeton, the rhetoric, symbolism, and metaphors used in athletics were used to frame these early debates. Debates were covered on front pages of college newspapers and emphasized in yearbooks, and team members even received the equivalent of athletic letters for their jackets. There even were rallies sending off the debating teams to matches. Yet, the debates never attained the broad appeal that athletics enjoyed. One reason may be that debates do not have a clear winner, as is the case in sports, and that scoring is subjective. In addition, with late 19th-century concerns about the impact of modern life on the human body, athletics offered hope that neither the individual nor the society was coming apart.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1909?10, football faced a crisis resulting from the failure of the previous reforms of 1905?06 to solve the problem of serious injuries. There was a mood of alarm and mistrust, and, while the crisis was developing, the presidents of Harvard, Yale, and Princeton developed a project to reform the sport and forestall possible radical changes forced by government upon the sport. President Arthur Hadley of Yale, A. Lawrence Lowell of Harvard, and Woodrow Wilson of Princeton worked to develop moderate changes to reduce injuries. Their attempts, however, were reduced by rebellion against the rules committee and formation of the Intercollegiate Athletic Association. The big three had tried to operate independently of the majority, but changes did reduce injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion">Expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Expansion">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg/220px-View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg/330px-View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg/440px-View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Connecticut_Hall_Old_Campus_Yale_College_New_Haven_Connecticut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Connecticut Hall, oldest building on the Yale campus, built between 1750 and 1753.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Yale expanded gradually, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Medicine" title="Yale School of Medicine">Yale School of Medicine</a> (1810), <a href="/wiki/Yale_Divinity_School" title="Yale Divinity School">Yale Divinity School</a> (1822), <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a> (1843), <a href="/wiki/Yale_Graduate_School_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Yale Graduate School of Arts and Sciences">Yale Graduate School of Arts and Sciences</a> (1847), the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Scientific_School" title="Sheffield Scientific School">Sheffield Scientific School</a> (1847),<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Art" title="Yale School of Art">Yale School of Fine Arts</a> (1869). In 1887, as the college continued to grow under the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_V" title="Timothy Dwight V">Timothy Dwight&nbsp;V</a>, Yale College was renamed Yale University. The university would later add the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Music" title="Yale School of Music">Yale School of Music</a> (1894), the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Forestry_%26_Environmental_Studies" title="Yale School of Forestry &amp; Environmental Studies">Yale School of Forestry &amp; Environmental Studies</a> (founded by <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">Gifford Pinchot</a> in 1901), the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Public_Health" title="Yale School of Public Health">Yale School of Public Health</a> (1915), the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Nursing" title="Yale School of Nursing">Yale School of Nursing</a> (1923), the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Drama" title="Yale School of Drama">Yale School of Drama</a> (1955), the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Physician_Associate_Program" title="Yale Physician Associate Program">Yale Physician Associate Program</a> (1973), and the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Management" title="Yale School of Management">Yale School of Management</a> (1976). It would also reorganize its relationship with the Sheffield Scientific School.</p> 
     <p>Expansion caused controversy about Yale's new roles. <a href="/wiki/Noah_Porter" title="Noah Porter">Noah Porter</a>, moral philosopher, was president from 1871 to 1886. During an age of tremendous expansion in higher education, Porter resisted the rise of the new research university, claiming that an eager embrace of its ideals would corrupt undergraduate education. Many of Porter's contemporaries criticized his administration, and historians since have disparaged his leadership. Levesque argues Porter was not a simple-minded reactionary, uncritically committed to tradition, but a principled and selective conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> He did not endorse everything old or reject everything new; rather, he sought to apply long-established ethical and pedagogical principles to a rapidly changing culture. He may have misunderstood some of the challenges of his time, but he correctly anticipated the enduring tensions that have accompanied the emergence and growth of the modern university.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/11/PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg/220px-PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/11/PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg/330px-PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/11/PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg/440px-PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PostcardNewHavenCTYaleUBirdsEye1911.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Aerial view from the south, 1906.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_sciences">Behavioral sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Behavioral sciences">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Between 1925 and 1940, <a href="/wiki/Foundation_(non-profit)" title="Foundation (non-profit)" class="mw-redirect">philanthropic foundations</a>, especially ones connected with the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_family" title="Rockefeller family">Rockefellers</a>, contributed about $7&nbsp;million to support the Yale Institute of Human Relations and the affiliated Yerkes Laboratories of <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">Primate</a> <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">Biology</a>. The money went toward behavioral science research, which was supported by foundation officers who aimed to &quot;improve mankind&quot; under an informal, loosely defined human engineering effort. The behavioral scientists at Yale, led by President <a href="/wiki/James_R._Angell" title="James R. Angell" class="mw-redirect">James R. Angell</a> and psychobiologist <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._Yerkes" title="Robert M. Yerkes" class="mw-redirect">Robert M. Yerkes</a>, tapped into foundation largesse by crafting research programs aimed to investigate, then suggest, ways to control, sexual and social behavior. For example, Yerkes analyzed chimpanzee sexual behavior in hopes of illuminating the evolutionary underpinnings of human development and providing information that could ameliorate dysfunction. Ultimately, the behavioral-science results disappointed foundation officers, who shifted their human-engineering funds toward biological sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Biology">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Slack (2003) compares three groups that conducted biological research at Yale during overlapping periods between 1910 and 1970. Yale proved important as a site for this research. The leaders of these groups were <a href="/wiki/Ross_Granville_Harrison" title="Ross Granville Harrison">Ross Granville Harrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grace_E._Pickford" title="Grace E. Pickford" class="mw-redirect">Grace E. Pickford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/G._Evelyn_Hutchinson" title="G. Evelyn Hutchinson">G.&nbsp;Evelyn Hutchinson</a>, and their members included both graduate students and more experienced scientists. All produced innovative research, including the opening of new subfields in embryology, endocrinology, and ecology, respectively, over a long period of time. Harrison's group is shown to have been a classic research school; Pickford's and Hutchinson's were not. Pickford's group was successful in spite of her lack of departmental or institutional position or power. Hutchinson and his graduate and postgraduate students were extremely productive, but in diverse areas of ecology rather than one focused area of research or the use of one set of research tools. Hutchinson's example shows that new models for research groups are needed, especially for those that include extensive field research.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Milton Winternitz led the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Medical_School" title="Yale Medical School" class="mw-redirect">Yale Medical School</a> as its dean from 1920 to 1935. An innovative, even maverick, leader, he not only kept the school from going under but also turned it into a first-class research institution.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Dedicated to the new scientific medicine established in Germany, he was equally fervent about &quot;social medicine&quot; and the study of humans in their culture and environment. He established the &quot;Yale System&quot; of teaching, with few lectures and fewer exams, and strengthened the full-time faculty system; he also created the graduate-level Yale School of Nursing and the Psychiatry Department, and built numerous new buildings. Progress toward his plans for an Institute of Human Relations, envisioned as a refuge where social scientists would collaborate with biological scientists in a holistic study of humankind, unfortunately lasted for only a few years before the opposition of resentful anti-Semitic colleagues drove him to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Faculty">Faculty</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Faculty">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Before <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War&nbsp;II</a>, most elite university faculties counted among their numbers few, if any, Jews, blacks, women, or other minorities; Yale was no exception. By 1980, this condition had been altered dramatically, as numerous members of those groups held faculty positions.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_American_Studies">History and American Studies</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: History and American Studies">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>The American studies program reflected the worldwide anti-Communist ideological struggle. <a href="/wiki/Norman_Holmes_Pearson" title="Norman Holmes Pearson">Norman Holmes Pearson</a>, who worked for the Office of Strategic Studies in London during World War II, returned to Yale and headed the new American studies program, in which scholarship quickly became an instrument of promoting liberty. Popular among undergraduates, the program sought to instruct them in the fundamentals of American civilization and thereby instill a sense of nationalism and national purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Also during the 1940s and 1950s, Wyoming millionaire William R. Coe made large contributions to the American studies programs at Yale University and at the University of Wyoming. Coe was concerned to celebrate the 'values' of the Western United States in order to meet the &quot;threat of communism.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Women studied at Yale University as early as 1892, in graduate-level programs at the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Graduate_School_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Yale Graduate School of Arts and Sciences">Yale Graduate School of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1966, Yale began discussions with its <a href="/wiki/Sister_school" title="Sister school">sister school</a> <a href="/wiki/Vassar_College" title="Vassar College">Vassar College</a> about merging to foster coeducation at the undergraduate level. Vassar, then all-female, declined the invitation. Both schools introduced coeducation independently in 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Amy Solomon was the first woman to register as a Yale undergraduate;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> she was also the first woman at Yale to join an undergraduate society, <a href="/wiki/St._Anthony_Hall" title="St. Anthony Hall">St. Anthony Hall</a>.</p> 
     <p>A decade into co-education, rampant student assault and harassment by faculty became the impetus for the trailblazing lawsuit <a href="/wiki/Alexander_v._Yale" title="Alexander v. Yale">Alexander v. Yale</a>. While unsuccessful in the courts, the legal reasoning behind the case changed the landscape of sex discrimination law and resulted in the establishment of Yale's Grievance Board and the Yale Women's Center.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In March 2011 a <a href="/wiki/Title_IX" title="Title IX">Title IX</a> complaint was filed against Yale.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Class">Class</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Class">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Yale, like other Ivy League schools, instituted policies in the early 20th century designed to increase the proportion of white Protestants of notable families in the student body (see <i><a href="/wiki/Numerus_clausus" title="Numerus clausus">numerus clausus</a></i>), and was one of the last of the Ivies to eliminate such preferences, beginning with the class of 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Town-gown_relations">Town-gown relations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Town-gown relations">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>Yale has a complicated relationship with its home city; for example, thousands of students volunteer every year in a myriad of community organizations, but city officials, who decry Yale's exemption from local property taxes, have long pressed the university to do more to help. Under President Levin, Yale has financially supported many of New Haven's efforts to reinvigorate the city. Evidence suggests that the <a href="/wiki/Town_and_gown" title="Town and gown">town and gown</a> relationships are mutually beneficial. Still, the economic power of the university increased dramatically with its financial success amid a decline in the local economy.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yale_in_the_21st_century">Yale in the 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Yale in the 21st century">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In 2006, Yale and <a href="/wiki/Peking_University" title="Peking University">Peking University</a> (PKU) established a Joint Undergraduate Program in Beijing, an <a href="/wiki/Exchange_program" title="Exchange program" class="mw-redirect">exchange program</a> allowing Yale students to spend a semester living and studying with PKU honor students.<sup id="cite_ref-yalePKU2012_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yalePKU2012-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 2012, the Peking University-Yale University Program ended due to weak participation.<sup id="cite_ref-yalePKU2012_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yalePKU2012-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2007 outgoing Yale President <a href="/wiki/Rick_Levin" title="Rick Levin">Rick Levin</a> characterized Yale's institutional priorities: &quot;First, among the nation's finest research universities, Yale is distinctively committed to excellence in undergraduate education. Second, in our graduate and professional schools, as well as in Yale College, we are committed to the education of leaders.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Globe" title="Boston Globe" class="mw-redirect">Boston Globe</a></i> wrote that &quot;if there's one school that can lay claim to educating the nation's top national leaders over the past three decades, it's Yale.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Magazine_p._6_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magazine_p._6-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yale_alumni" title="Yale alumni" class="mw-redirect">Yale alumni</a> were represented on the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> ticket in every U.S. Presidential election between 1972 and 2004. Yale-educated Presidents since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> include <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_H.W._Bush" title="George H.W. Bush" class="mw-redirect">George H.W. Bush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>, and major-party nominees during this period include <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> (2004), <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lieberman" title="Joseph Lieberman" class="mw-redirect">Joseph Lieberman</a> (Vice President, 2000), and <a href="/wiki/Sargent_Shriver" title="Sargent Shriver">Sargent Shriver</a> (Vice President, 1972). Other Yale alumni who made serious bids for the Presidency during this period include <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Rodham_Clinton" title="Hillary Rodham Clinton">Hillary Rodham Clinton</a> (2008), <a href="/wiki/Howard_Dean" title="Howard Dean">Howard Dean</a> (2004), <a href="/wiki/Gary_Hart" title="Gary Hart">Gary Hart</a> (1984 and 1988), <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tsongas" title="Paul Tsongas">Paul Tsongas</a> (1992), <a href="/wiki/Pat_Robertson" title="Pat Robertson">Pat Robertson</a> (1988) and <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a> (1976, 1980, 1992).</p> 
     <p>Several explanations have been offered for Yale?s representation in national elections since the end of the Vietnam War. Various sources note the spirit of campus activism that has existed at Yale since the 1960s, and the intellectual influence of Reverend <a href="/wiki/William_Sloane_Coffin" title="William Sloane Coffin">William Sloane Coffin</a> on many of the future candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale President Richard Levin attributes the run to Yale?s focus on creating &quot;a laboratory for future leaders,&quot; an institutional priority that began during the tenure of Yale Presidents <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Whitney_Griswold" title="Alfred Whitney Griswold">Alfred Whitney Griswold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingman_Brewster" title="Kingman Brewster" class="mw-redirect">Kingman Brewster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Brodhead" title="Richard H. Brodhead">Richard H. Brodhead</a>, former dean of Yale College and now president of <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>, stated: &quot;We do give very significant attention to orientation to the community in our admissions, and there is a very strong tradition of <a href="/wiki/Volunteerism" title="Volunteerism" class="mw-redirect">volunteerism</a> at Yale.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Magazine_p._6_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magazine_p._6-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale historian <a href="/wiki/Gaddis_Smith" title="Gaddis Smith">Gaddis Smith</a> notes &quot;an ethos of organized activity&quot; at Yale during the 20th century that led John Kerry to lead the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Political_Union" title="Yale Political Union">Yale Political Union</a>'s Liberal Party, <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a> the Conservative Party, and Joseph Lieberman to manage the <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Daily_News" title="Yale Daily News">Yale Daily News</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camille_Paglia" title="Camille Paglia">Camille Paglia</a> points to a history of networking and elitism: &quot;It has to do with a web of friendships and affiliations built up in school.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> CNN suggests that George W. Bush benefited from preferential admissions policies for the &quot;son and grandson of alumni&quot;, and for a &quot;member of a politically influential family.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times" class="mw-redirect">New York Times</a></i> correspondent <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Bumiller" title="Elisabeth Bumiller">Elisabeth Bumiller</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic_Monthly" title="The Atlantic Monthly" class="mw-redirect">The Atlantic Monthly</a></i> correspondent <a href="/wiki/James_Fallows" title="James Fallows">James Fallows</a> credit the culture of community and cooperation that exists between students, faculty, and administration, which downplays self-interest and reinforces commitment to others.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the 1988 presidential election, <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H.&nbsp;W. Bush</a> (Yale '48) derided <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">Michael Dukakis</a> for having &quot;foreign-policy views born in Harvard Yard's boutique&quot;. When challenged on the distinction between Dukakis's Harvard connection and his own Yale background, he said that, unlike Harvard, Yale's reputation was &quot;so diffuse, there isn't a symbol, I don't think, in the Yale situation, any symbolism in it&quot; and said Yale did not share Harvard's reputation for &quot;liberalism and elitism&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2004 <a href="/wiki/Howard_Dean" title="Howard Dean">Howard Dean</a> stated, &quot;In some ways, I consider myself separate from the other three (Yale) candidates of 2004. Yale changed so much between the class of '68 and the class of '71. My class was the first class to have women in it; it was the first class to have a significant effort to recruit African Americans. It was an extraordinary time, and in that span of time is the change of an entire generation&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2009, former <a href="/wiki/British_Prime_Minister" title="British Prime Minister" class="mw-redirect">British Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> picked Yale as one location ? the others are Britain's <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universiti_Teknologi_Mara" title="Universiti Teknologi Mara" class="mw-redirect">Universiti Teknologi Mara</a> ? for the <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair_Faith_Foundation" title="Tony Blair Faith Foundation">Tony Blair Faith Foundation</a>'s United States Faith and Globalization Initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2009, former Mexican President <a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Zedillo" title="Ernesto Zedillo">Ernesto Zedillo</a> is the director of the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Center_for_the_Study_of_Globalization" title="Yale Center for the Study of Globalization">Yale Center for the Study of Globalization</a> and teaches an undergraduate seminar, &quot;Debating Globalization&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2009, former presidential candidate and DNC chair <a href="/wiki/Howard_Dean" title="Howard Dean">Howard Dean</a> teaches a residential college seminar, &quot;Understanding Politics and Politicians.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Also in 2009, an alliance was formed among Yale, <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>, and both schools? affiliated hospital complexes to conduct research focused on the direct improvement of patient care?a growing field known as translational medicine. President Richard Levin noted that Yale has hundreds of other partnerships across the world, but &quot;no existing collaboration matches the scale of the new partnership with UCL&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>New international Yale initiatives launched included (among many others):</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Jackson Institute for Global Affairs, promoting international education University-wide;</li> 
      <li>Global Health Initiative, uniting and expanding global health efforts across campus;</li> 
      <li>Yale India Initiative, expanding the study of and engagement with India;</li> 
      <li>Yale Center for the Study of Globalization, bridging the gap between academia and the world of public policy; and</li> 
      <li>Yale China Law Center, promoting the rule of law in China.</li> 
      <li>New global research and educational partnerships included (among many others):</li> 
      <li>Yale-Universidad de Chile International Program in Astronomy Education and Research;</li> 
      <li>Peking-Yale Joint Center for Plant Molecular Genetics and Agrobiology;</li> 
      <li>Todai?Yale Initiative for the Study of Japan;</li> 
      <li>Fudan-Yale Biomedical Research Center in Shanghai;</li> 
      <li>Yale-University College London Collaboration; and</li> 
      <li>UNSAAC-Yale Center for the Study of Machu Picchu and Inca Culture in Peru.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>The most ambitious international partnership to date is <a href="/wiki/Yale-NUS_College" title="Yale-NUS College" class="mw-redirect">Yale-NUS College</a> in Singapore, a joint effort with the National University of Singapore to create a new liberal arts college in Asia featuring an innovative curriculum that weaves Western and Asian traditions, set to open in August 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT1_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_organization">Administration and organization</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Administration and organization">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin-left:1em; font-size:90%; line-height:1.4em; width:280px;">
