     <p>The <a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Yale_College" title="President and Fellows of Yale College" class="mw-redirect">President and Fellows of Yale College</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Corporation" title="Yale Corporation">Yale Corporation</a>, is the governing board of the University.</p> 
     <p>Yale's president <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._Levin" title="Richard C. Levin" class="mw-redirect">Richard C. Levin</a> is one of the highest paid university presidents in the United States with a 2008 salary of $1.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Yale Provost's Office has launched several women into prominent university presidencies. In 1977 <a href="/wiki/Hanna_Holborn_Gray" title="Hanna Holborn Gray">Hanna Holborn Gray</a> was appointed acting President of Yale from this position, and went on to become President of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, the first woman to be full president of a major university. In 1994 Yale Provost <a href="/wiki/Judith_Rodin" title="Judith Rodin">Judith Rodin</a> became the first female president of an Ivy League institution at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>. In 2002 Provost <a href="/wiki/Alison_Richard" title="Alison Richard">Alison Richard</a> became the Vice Chancellor of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>. In 2004, Provost <a href="/wiki/Susan_Hockfield" title="Susan Hockfield">Susan Hockfield</a> became the President of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a>. In 2007 Deputy Provost Kim Bottomly was named President of <a href="/wiki/Wellesley_College" title="Wellesley College">Wellesley College</a>. In 2003, the Dean of the Divinity School, <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_Chopp" title="Rebecca Chopp">Rebecca Chopp</a>, was appointed president of Colgate University and now heads Swarthmore College.</p> 
     <p>In 2008 Provost <a href="/wiki/Andrew_D._Hamilton" title="Andrew D. Hamilton">Andrew Hamilton</a> was confirmed to be the Vice Chancellor of the University of Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Former Dean of Yale College <a href="/wiki/Richard_H._Brodhead" title="Richard H. Brodhead">Richard H. Brodhead</a> serves as the President of <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Staff_and_labor_unions">Staff and labor unions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Staff and labor unions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Hospital_and_University_Employees" title="Federation of Hospital and University Employees">Federation of Hospital and University Employees</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Much of Yale University's staff, including most maintenance staff, dining hall employees, and administrative staff, are <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">unionized</a>. <a href="/wiki/Clerk" title="Clerk">Clerical</a> and technical employees are represented by Local&nbsp;34 of <a href="/wiki/Unite_Here" title="Unite Here" class="mw-redirect">Unite Here</a> and service and maintenance workers by Local&nbsp;35 of the same <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">international</a>. Together with the <a href="/wiki/Graduate_Employees_and_Students_Organization" title="Graduate Employees and Students Organization">Graduate Employees and Students Organization</a> (GESO), an unrecognized union of graduate employees, Locals&nbsp;34 and 35 make up the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Hospital_and_University_Employees" title="Federation of Hospital and University Employees">Federation of Hospital and University Employees</a>. Also included in FHUE are the dietary workers at <a href="/wiki/Yale-New_Haven_Hospital" title="Yale-New Haven Hospital" class="mw-redirect">Yale-New Haven Hospital</a>, who are members of <a href="/wiki/1199SEIU_United_Healthcare_Workers_East" title="1199SEIU United Healthcare Workers East">1199 SEIU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition to these unions, officers of the Yale University Police Department are members of the Yale Police Benevolent Association, which affiliated in 2005 with the Connecticut Organization for Public Safety Employees.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Finally, Yale security officers voted to join the <a href="/wiki/Security_Guard#Security.2C_Police.2C_and_Fire_Professionals_of_America" title="Security Guard" class="mw-redirect">International Union of Security, Police and Fire Professionals of America</a> in fall 2010 after the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Board" title="National Labor Relations Board">National Labor Relations Board</a> ruled they could not join <a href="/wiki/AFSCME" title="AFSCME" class="mw-redirect">AFSCME</a>; the Yale administration contested the election.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale has a history of difficult and prolonged labor negotiations, often culminating in strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> There have been at least eight strikes since 1968, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> wrote that Yale has a reputation as having the worst record of labor tension of any university in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale's unusually large endowment exacerbates the tension over wages. Moreover, Yale has been accused of failing to treat workers with respect.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> In a 2003 strike, however, the university claimed that more union employees were working than striking.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/David_Graeber" title="David Graeber">David Graeber</a> was 'retired' after he came to the defense of a student who was involved in campus labor issues.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg/220px-Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg/330px-Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg/440px-Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_Law_School_in_the_Sterling_Law_Building.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Yale_Law_School" title="Yale Law School">Yale Law School</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Yale's central campus in <a href="/wiki/Downtown_New_Haven" title="Downtown New Haven">downtown New Haven</a> covers 260 acres (1.1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>). An additional 500 acres (2.0&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) includes the <a href="/wiki/Yale_golf_course" title="Yale golf course">Yale golf course</a> and nature preserves in rural Connecticut and <a href="/wiki/Thimble_Islands" title="Thimble Islands">Horse Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale is noted for its largely <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_Gothic" title="Collegiate Gothic" class="mw-redirect">Collegiate Gothic</a> campus<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as for several iconic modern buildings commonly discussed in architectural history survey courses: <a href="/wiki/Louis_Kahn" title="Louis Kahn">Louis Kahn</a>'s Yale Art Gallery<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> and Center for British Art, <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>'s Ingalls Rink and Ezra Stiles and Morse Colleges, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Rudolph_(architect)" title="Paul Rudolph (architect)">Paul Rudolph's</a> <a href="/wiki/Yale_Art_and_Architecture_Building" title="Yale Art and Architecture Building">Art &amp; Architecture Building</a>. Yale also owns and has restored many noteworthy 19th-century mansions along <a href="/wiki/Hillhouse_Avenue" title="Hillhouse Avenue">Hillhouse Avenue</a>, which was considered the most beautiful street in America by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> when he visited the United States in the 1840s.</p> 
     <p>Many of Yale's buildings were constructed in the <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_Gothic" title="Collegiate Gothic" class="mw-redirect">Collegiate Gothic</a> architecture style from 1917 to 1931, financed largely by <a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Harkness" title="Edward S. Harkness" class="mw-redirect">Edward S. Harkness</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Stone sculpture built into the walls of the buildings portray contemporary college personalities such as a writer, an athlete, a tea-drinking socialite, and a student who has fallen asleep while reading. Similarly, the decorative <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">friezes</a> on the buildings depict contemporary scenes such as policemen chasing a robber and arresting a prostitute (on the wall of the Law School), or a student relaxing with a mug of beer and a cigarette. The architect, <a href="/wiki/James_Gamble_Rogers" title="James Gamble Rogers">James Gamble Rogers</a>, faux-aged these buildings by splashing the walls with acid,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> deliberately breaking their <a href="/wiki/Lead_glass" title="Lead glass">leaded glass</a> windows and repairing them in the style of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, and creating niches for decorative statuary but leaving them empty to simulate loss or theft over the ages. In fact, the buildings merely simulate Middle Ages architecture, for though they appear to be constructed of solid stone blocks in the authentic manner, most actually have steel framing as was commonly used in 1930. One exception is <a href="/wiki/Harkness_Tower" title="Harkness Tower">Harkness Tower</a>, 216 feet (66&nbsp;m) tall, which was originally a free-standing stone structure. It was reinforced in 1964 to allow the installation of the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Memorial_Carillon" title="Yale Memorial Carillon">Yale Memorial Carillon</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg/220px-Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg/330px-Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg/440px-Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vanderbilt_Hall_at_Yale_university.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_family" title="Vanderbilt family">Vanderbilt Hall</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Other examples of the Gothic (also called neo-Gothic and collegiate Gothic) style are on <a href="/wiki/Old_Campus" title="Old Campus">Old Campus</a> by such architects as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Austin_(architect)" title="Henry Austin (architect)">Henry Austin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_C._Haight" title="Charles C. Haight">Charles C. Haight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russell_Sturgis" title="Russell Sturgis">Russell Sturgis</a>. Several are associated with members of the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_family" title="Vanderbilt family">Vanderbilt family</a>, including Vanderbilt Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Phelps Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/St._Anthony_Hall" title="St. Anthony Hall">St. Anthony Hall</a> (a commission for member <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_Vanderbilt" title="Frederick William Vanderbilt">Frederick William Vanderbilt</a>), the Mason, Sloane and Osborn laboratories, dormitories for the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Scientific_School" title="Sheffield Scientific School">Sheffield Scientific School</a> (the engineering and sciences school at Yale until 1956) and elements of <a href="/wiki/Silliman_College" title="Silliman College">Silliman College</a>, the largest residential college.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:NathanHaleStatue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/NathanHaleStatue.jpg/170px-NathanHaleStatue.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/NathanHaleStatue.jpg/255px-NathanHaleStatue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bc/NathanHaleStatue.jpg/340px-NathanHaleStatue.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:NathanHaleStatue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Statue of 
        <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Hale" title="Nathan Hale">Nathan Hale</a> in front of 
        <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Hall" title="Connecticut Hall">Connecticut Hall</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The oldest building on campus, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Hall" title="Connecticut Hall">Connecticut Hall</a> (built in 1750), is in the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian style</a>. Georgian-style buildings erected from 1929 to 1933 include <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_College" title="Timothy Dwight College">Timothy Dwight College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierson_College" title="Pierson College">Pierson College</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Davenport_College" title="Davenport College">Davenport College</a>, except the latter's east, York Street fa&ccedil;ade, which was constructed in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic style</a>.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_and_Manuscript_Library" title="Beinecke Rare Book and Manuscript Library">Beinecke Rare Book and Manuscript Library</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Bunshaft" title="Gordon Bunshaft">Gordon Bunshaft</a> of <a href="/wiki/Skidmore,_Owings,_and_Merrill" title="Skidmore, Owings, and Merrill" class="mw-redirect">Skidmore, Owings, and Merrill</a>, is one of the largest buildings in the world reserved exclusively for the preservation of rare books and manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> It is located near the center of the University in <a href="/wiki/Hewitt_Quadrangle" title="Hewitt Quadrangle">Hewitt Quadrangle</a>, which is now more commonly referred to as &quot;<a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Plaza" title="Beinecke Plaza" class="mw-redirect">Beinecke Plaza</a>&quot;.</p> 
     <p>The library's six-story above-ground tower of book stacks is surrounded by a windowless rectangular building with walls made of translucent Vermont marble, which transmit subdued lighting to the interior and provide protection from direct light, while glowing from within after dark.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:BeineckeInterior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/BeineckeInterior.jpg/170px-BeineckeInterior.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/BeineckeInterior.jpg/255px-BeineckeInterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/BeineckeInterior.jpg/340px-BeineckeInterior.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:BeineckeInterior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Library" title="Beinecke Library" class="mw-redirect">Interior of Beinecke Library</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The sculptures in the sunken courtyard by <a href="/wiki/Isamu_Noguchi" title="Isamu Noguchi">Isamu Noguchi</a> are said to represent time (the pyramid), the sun (the circle), and chance (the cube).</p> 
     <p>Alumnus <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>, Finnish-American architect of such notable structures as the <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Arch" title="Gateway Arch">Gateway Arch</a> in St.&nbsp;Louis, <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">Washington Dulles International Airport</a> main terminal, <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs_Holmdel_Complex" title="Bell Labs Holmdel Complex">Bell Labs Holmdel Complex</a> and the <a href="/wiki/CBS_Building" title="CBS Building">CBS Building</a> in Manhattan, designed <a href="/wiki/Ingalls_Rink" title="Ingalls Rink">Ingalls Rink</a> at Yale and the newest residential colleges of Ezra Stiles and Morse. These latter were modelled after the medieval Italian hilltown of <a href="/wiki/San_Gimignano" title="San Gimignano">San Gimignano</a> ? a prototype chosen for the town's pedestrian-friendly milieu and fortress-like stone towers. These tower forms at Yale act in counterpoint to the college's many Gothic spires and Georgian cupolas.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale's Office of Sustainability develops and implements sustainability practices at Yale.<sup id="cite_ref-Yale_Sustainability_Strategy_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale_Sustainability_Strategy-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale is committed to reduce its greenhouse gas emissions 10% below 1990 levels by the year 2020. As part of this commitment, the university allocates renewable energy credits to offset some of the energy used by residential colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-Yale_commits_to_long-term_Greenhouse_Gas_Reduction_and_Renewable_Energy_Strategy_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale_commits_to_long-term_Greenhouse_Gas_Reduction_and_Renewable_Energy_Strategy-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Eleven campus buildings are candidates for LEED design and certification.<sup id="cite_ref-Yale.E2.80.99s_Greenhouse_Gas_Reduction_Strategy_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale.E2.80.99s_Greenhouse_Gas_Reduction_Strategy-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale Sustainable Food Project initiated the introduction of local, organic vegetables, fruits, and beef to all residential college dining halls.<sup id="cite_ref-Yale_Sustainable_Food_Project_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yale_Sustainable_Food_Project-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale was listed as a Campus Sustainability Leader on the Sustainable Endowments Institute?s College Sustainability Report Card 2008, and received a ?B+? grade overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Grove_Street_Cemetery,_New_Haven" title="Grove Street Cemetery, New Haven" class="mw-redirect">Grove Street Cemetery, New Haven</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Marsh_Botanical_Garden" title="Marsh Botanical Garden">Marsh Botanical Garden</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Yale_Sustainable_Food_Project" title="Yale Sustainable Food Project">Yale Sustainable Food Project Farm</a></li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:1008px;"> 
      <div class="thumbinner"> 
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard,_Yale_University,_New_Haven,_CT,_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg/1000px-Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg" width="1000" height="324" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg/1500px-Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg/2000px-Yale_Old_Campus_Courtyard%2C_Yale_University%2C_New_Haven%2C_CT%2C_USA_-_Dec_2012.jpg 2x" /></a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_nonresidential_campus_buildings">Notable nonresidential campus buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notable nonresidential campus buildings">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Notable nonresidential campus buildings and landmarks include <a href="/wiki/Battell_Chapel" title="Battell Chapel">Battell Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_Library" title="Beinecke Rare Book Library" class="mw-redirect">Beinecke Rare Book Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harkness_Tower" title="Harkness Tower">Harkness Tower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ingalls_Rink" title="Ingalls Rink">Ingalls Rink</a>, Kline Biology Tower, <a href="/wiki/Osborne_Memorial_Laboratories" title="Osborne Memorial Laboratories" class="mw-redirect">Osborne Memorial Laboratories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Payne_Whitney_Gymnasium" title="Payne Whitney Gymnasium">Payne Whitney Gymnasium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">Peabody Museum of Natural History</a>, Sterling Hall of Medicine, <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Law_Buildings" title="Sterling Law Buildings" class="mw-redirect">Sterling Law Buildings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Memorial_Library" title="Sterling Memorial Library">Sterling Memorial Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woolsey_Hall" title="Woolsey Hall">Woolsey Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_Center_for_British_Art" title="Yale Center for British Art">Yale Center for British Art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Art_Gallery" title="Yale University Art Gallery">Yale University Art Gallery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yale_Art_%26_Architecture_Building" title="Yale Art &amp; Architecture Building" class="mw-redirect">Yale Art &amp; Architecture Building</a>.</p> 
     <p>Yale's secret society buildings (some of which are called &quot;tombs&quot;) were built both to be private yet unmistakable. A diversity of architectural styles is represented: <a href="/wiki/Berzelius_(secret_society)" title="Berzelius (secret society)">Berzelius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donn_Barber" title="Donn Barber">Donn Barber</a> in an austere cube with classical detailing (erected in 1908 or 1910); <a href="/wiki/Book_and_Snake" title="Book and Snake">Book and Snake</a>, Louis R. Metcalfe in a <a href="/wiki/Greek_Ionic" title="Greek Ionic" class="mw-redirect">Greek Ionic</a> style (erected in 1901); <a href="/wiki/Elihu_(secret_society)" title="Elihu (secret society)">Elihu</a>, architect unknown but built in a <a href="/wiki/American_colonial_architecture" title="American colonial architecture">Colonial</a> style (constructed on an early 17th-century foundation although the building is from the 18th century); <a href="/wiki/Mace_and_Chain" title="Mace and Chain">Mace and Chain</a>, in a late colonial, early <a href="/wiki/Victorian_fashion" title="Victorian fashion">Victorian style</a> (built in 1823). Interior moulding is said to have belonged to <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a>; <a href="/wiki/Manuscript_Society" title="Manuscript Society">Manuscript Society</a>, King Lui-Wu with Dan Kniley responsible for landscaping and <a href="/wiki/Josef_Albers" title="Josef Albers">Josef Albers</a> for the brickwork intaglio mural. Building constructed in a <a href="/wiki/Mid-century_modern" title="Mid-century modern" class="mw-redirect">mid-century modern</a> style; <a href="/wiki/Scroll_and_Key" title="Scroll and Key">Scroll and Key</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Morris_Hunt" title="Richard Morris Hunt">Richard Morris Hunt</a> in a Moorish- or Islamic-inspired <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts style</a> (erected 1869?70); <a href="/wiki/Skull_and_Bones" title="Skull and Bones">Skull and Bones</a>, possibly <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Jackson_Davis" title="Alexander Jackson Davis">Alexander Jackson Davis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Henry_Austin_(architect)" title="Henry Austin (architect)">Henry Austin</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revival" title="Egyptian Revival" class="mw-redirect">Egypto-Doric style</a> utilizing <a href="/wiki/Brownstone" title="Brownstone">Brownstone</a> (in 1856 the first wing was completed, in 1903 the second wing, 1911 the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Gothic" title="Neo-Gothic" class="mw-redirect">Neo-Gothic</a> towers in rear garden were completed); <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo_(secret_society)" title="St. Elmo (secret society)">St. Elmo</a>, (former tomb) <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_MacKenzie_Murchison" title="Kenneth MacKenzie Murchison">Kenneth M. Murchison</a>, 1912, designs inspired by Elizabethan manor. Current location, brick colonial; and <a href="/wiki/Wolf%27s_Head_(secret_society)" title="Wolf's Head (secret society)">Wolf's Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Grosvenor_Goodhue" title="Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue" class="mw-redirect">Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue</a> (erected 1923-4).</p>

     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus_safety">Campus safety</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Campus safety">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>In addition to the Yale University Police Department, founded in 1894,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> a variety of safety services are available including blue phones, a <a href="/wiki/Safety_escort" title="Safety escort" class="mw-redirect">safety escort</a>, and a shuttle service.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the 1970s and 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="Connecticut locations by per capita income" class="mw-redirect">poverty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Violent_crime" title="Violent crime">violent crime</a> rose in New Haven, dampening Yale's student and faculty recruiting efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Between 1990 and 2006, New Haven's crime rate fell by half, helped by a community policing strategy by the New Haven police and Yale's campus became the safest among the Ivy League and other peer schools.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, across the board, the city of New Haven has retained the highest levels of crime of any Ivy League city for more than a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Between 2002 and 2004, Yale reported 14 violent crimes (<a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a>, aggravated <a href="/wiki/Assault" title="Assault">assault</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sex_offender" title="Sex offender">sex offenses</a>); in comparison, Harvard reported 83 such incidents, Princeton 24, and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford</a> 54. The incidence of nonviolent crime (<a href="/wiki/Burglary" title="Burglary">burglary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">arson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle_theft" title="Motor vehicle theft">motor vehicle theft</a>) was also lower than most of its peer schools.</p> 
     <p>In 2004 a national non-profit watchdog group called Security on Campus filed a complaint with the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Education" title="U.S. Department of Education" class="mw-redirect">U.S.&nbsp;Department of Education</a>, accusing Yale of under-reporting rape and sexual assaults.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Admissions">Admissions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Admissions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Sterling_Memorial_Library_2,_September_1,_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg/170px-Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg/255px-Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg/340px-Sterling_Memorial_Library_2%2C_September_1%2C_2008.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Sterling_Memorial_Library_2,_September_1,_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Yale University's 
        <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Memorial_Library" title="Sterling Memorial Library">Sterling Memorial Library</a>, as seen from 
        <a href="/wiki/Maya_Lin" title="Maya Lin">Maya Lin's</a> sculpture, 
        <i>Women's Table</i>. The sculpture records the number of women enrolled at Yale over its history; female undergraduates were not admitted until 1969.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>For the Class of 2017, Yale accepted 1,991 students out of a record 29,610 total applications, hitting a record-low acceptance rate of 6.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Through its program of need-based financial aid, Yale commits to meet the full demonstrated financial need of all applicants. Most financial aid is in the form of grants and scholarships that do not need to be paid back to the university, and the average need-based aid grant for the Class of 2016 was $41,320. Over 10% of students in Yale College are expected to have $0 parental contribution.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Half of all Yale undergraduates are women, more than 30% are <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_minority" title="Ethnic minority" class="mw-redirect">minorities</a>, and 8% are <a href="/wiki/International_student" title="International student">international students</a>. Fifty-five percent attended public schools and 45% attended private, religious, or international schools.<sup id="cite_ref-2010_stats_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010_stats-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition, Yale College admits a small group of non-traditional students each year, through the <a href="/wiki/Eli_Whitney_Students_Program" title="Eli Whitney Students Program">Eli Whitney Students Program</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collections">Collections</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Collections">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_(The_Night_Caf%C3%A9)_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg/220px-Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg/330px-Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg/440px-Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_%28The_Night_Caf%C3%A9%29_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Le_caf%C3%A9_de_nuit_(The_Night_Caf%C3%A9)_by_Vincent_van_Gogh.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Caf%C3%A9" title="The Night Caf&eacute;">The Night Caf&eacute;</a></i>, Vincent van Gogh, 1888, 
        <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Art_Gallery" title="Yale University Art Gallery">Yale Art Gallery</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Library" title="Yale University Library">Yale University Library</a>, which holds over 12 million volumes, is the second-largest university collection in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The main library, <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Memorial_Library" title="Sterling Memorial Library">Sterling Memorial Library</a>, contains about 4&nbsp;million volumes, and other holdings are dispersed at subject libraries.</p> 
     <p>Rare books are found in a number of Yale collections. The <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_Library" title="Beinecke Rare Book Library" class="mw-redirect">Beinecke Rare Book Library</a> has a large collection of rare books and manuscripts. The <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Cushing/John_Hay_Whitney_Medical_Library" title="Harvey Cushing/John Hay Whitney Medical Library">Harvey Cushing/John Hay Whitney Medical Library</a> includes important historical medical texts, including an impressive collection of rare books, as well as historical medical instruments. The <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Walpole_Library" title="Lewis Walpole Library">Lewis Walpole Library</a> contains the largest collection of 18th?century British literary works. The <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Club" title="Elizabethan Club">Elizabethan Club</a>, technically a private organization, makes its Elizabethan folios and first editions available to qualified researchers through Yale.</p> 
     <p>Yale's museum collections are also of international stature. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Art_Gallery" title="Yale University Art Gallery">Yale University Art Gallery</a> is the country's first university-affiliated art museum. It contains more than 180,000 works, including old masters and important collections of modern art, in the Swartout and Kahn buildings. The latter, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Kahn" title="Louis Kahn">Louis Kahn</a>'s first large-scale American work (1953), was renovated and reopened in December 2006. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_Center_for_British_Art" title="Yale Center for British Art">Yale Center for British Art</a>, the largest collection of British art outside of the UK, grew from a gift of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Mellon" title="Paul Mellon">Paul Mellon</a> and is housed in another Kahn-designed building.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">Peabody Museum of Natural History</a> in New Haven is used by school children and contains research collections in anthropology, archaeology, and the natural environment. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_University_Collection_of_Musical_Instruments" title="Yale University Collection of Musical Instruments">Yale University Collection of Musical Instruments</a>, affiliated with the Yale School of Music, is perhaps the least well-known of Yale's collections, because its hours of opening are restricted.</p> 
     <p>The museums also house the artifacts brought to the United States from <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> by Yale history professor <a href="/wiki/Hiram_Bingham_III" title="Hiram Bingham III">Hiram Bingham</a> in his expedition to <a href="/wiki/Machu_Picchu" title="Machu Picchu">Machu Picchu</a> in 1912 ? when the removal of such artifacts was legal. Peru would now like to have the items returned; Yale has so far declined.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> In November 2010, a Yale University representative agreed to return the artifacts to a Peruvian university.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_rankings">University rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: University rankings">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width: 22em">

     </table> 
     <p>The <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> ranked Yale third among national universities in 2012, as it has for each of the past fifteen years, in every case behind Princeton and Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> It was ranked fourth in the 2011 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS&nbsp;World University Rankings</a> and tenth in the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-World_University_Rankings_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-World_University_Rankings-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Shanghai Jiao Tong University?s <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a>, placed Yale at 11 in 2010. ARWU also ranked Yale 25th in Natural Sciences and Mathematics, 76?100th in Engineering/Technology and Computer Sciences, 9th in Life and Agriculture Sciences, 21st in Clinical Medicine and Pharmacy, and 8th in Social Sciences worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Faculty.2C_research.2C_and_intellectual_traditions">Faculty, research, and intellectual traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Faculty, research, and intellectual traditions">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>The college is, after normalization for institution size, the tenth-largest baccalaureate source of <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctoral degree</a> recipients in the United States, and the largest such source within the Ivy League.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale's English and Comparative Literature departments were part of the <a href="/wiki/New_Criticism" title="New Criticism">New Criticism</a> movement. Of the New Critics, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Penn_Warren" title="Robert Penn Warren">Robert Penn Warren</a>, <a href="/wiki/W.K._Wimsatt" title="W.K. Wimsatt" class="mw-redirect">W.K. Wimsatt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cleanth_Brooks" title="Cleanth Brooks">Cleanth Brooks</a> were all Yale faculty. Later, the Yale Comparative literature department became a center of American <a href="/wiki/Deconstruction" title="Deconstruction">deconstruction</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">Jacques Derrida</a>, the father of deconstruction, taught at the Department of Comparative Literature from the late seventies to mid-1980s. Several other Yale faculty members were also associated with deconstruction, forming the so-called &quot;<a href="/wiki/Yale_school_(deconstruction)" title="Yale school (deconstruction)" class="mw-redirect">Yale School</a>&quot;. These included <a href="/wiki/Paul_de_Man" title="Paul de Man">Paul de Man</a> who taught in the Departments of Comparative Literature and French, <a href="/wiki/J._Hillis_Miller" title="J. Hillis Miller">J.&nbsp;Hillis Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Hartman" title="Geoffrey Hartman">Geoffrey Hartman</a> (both taught in the Departments of English and Comparative Literature), and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a> (English), whose theoretical position was always somewhat specific, and who ultimately took a very different path from the rest of this group. Yale's history department has also originated important intellectual trends. Historians <a href="/wiki/C._Vann_Woodward" title="C. Vann Woodward">C.&nbsp;Vann Woodward</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Brion_Davis" title="David Brion Davis">David Brion Davis</a> are credited with beginning in the 1960s and 1970s an important stream of <a href="/wiki/American_South" title="American South" class="mw-redirect">southern</a> historians; likewise, <a href="/wiki/David_Montgomery_(historian)" title="David Montgomery (historian)">David Montgomery</a>, a labor historian, advised many of the current generation of labor historians in the country. Yale's Music School and Department fostered the growth of Music Theory in the latter half of the 20th&nbsp;century. The <i>Journal of Music Theory</i> was founded there in 1957; <a href="/wiki/Allen_Forte" title="Allen Forte">Allen Forte</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Lewin" title="David Lewin">David Lewin</a> were influential teachers and scholars.</p> 
     <p>Since summer 2010, Yale has also been host to <a href="/wiki/Yale_Publishing_Course" title="Yale Publishing Course">Yale Publishing Course</a>.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus_life">Campus life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Campus life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Yale is a medium-sized research university, most of whose students are in the <a href="/wiki/Graduate_school" title="Graduate school">graduate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Professional_school" title="Professional school">professional</a> schools. <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">Undergraduates</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Yale_College" title="Yale College">Yale College</a> students, come from a variety of ethnic, national, and socioeconomic backgrounds. Of the 2010?2011 freshman class, 10% are non?U.S. citizens, while 54% went to public high schools.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale is also an open campus for the <a href="/wiki/Gay_community" title="Gay community" class="mw-redirect">gay community</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Its active LGBT community first received wide publicity in the late 1980s, when Yale obtained a reputation as the &quot;gay Ivy&quot;, due largely to a 1987 <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal" class="mw-redirect">Wall Street Journal</a></i> article written by Julie V. Iovine, an alumna and the spouse of a Yale faculty member. During the same year, the University hosted a national conference on gay and lesbian studies and established the Lesbian and Gay Studies Center.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> The slogan &quot;One in Four, Maybe More&quot; was coined by the campus gay community. While the community in the 1980s and early 1990s was very activist, today most LGBT events have become part of the general campus social scene.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, the annual LGBT Co-op Dance attracts straight as well as gay students.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residential_colleges">Residential colleges</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Residential colleges">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Residential_colleges_of_Yale_University" title="Residential colleges of Yale University">Residential colleges of Yale University</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Yale has a system of 12 <a href="/wiki/Residential_college" title="Residential college">residential colleges</a>, instituted in 1933 through a grant by Yale graduate <a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Harkness" title="Edward S. Harkness" class="mw-redirect">Edward S. Harkness</a>, who admired the college systems at Oxford and Cambridge. Each college has a Dean, Master, affiliated faculty, and resident Fellows. Each college also features distinctive architecture, secluded courtyards, a commons room, meeting rooms/classrooms, and a dining hall; in addition some have chapels, libraries, <a href="/wiki/Squash_(sport)" title="Squash (sport)">squash</a> courts, pool tables, short order dining counters, cafes, or darkrooms. Each college at Yale offers its own seminars, social events, and Master's Teas, and most of them are open to students from other residential colleges. However, Yale remains a unitary university, while Oxford and Cambridge colleges are self-governed charitable institutions in their own right.</p> 
     <p>All of Yale's 2,000 undergraduate courses are open to members of any college.</p> 
     <p>The dominant architecture of the residential colleges is <a href="/wiki/Neo-Gothic" title="Neo-Gothic" class="mw-redirect">Neo-Gothic</a>, in line with the characteristic architecture of the university. Several colleges have other period architecture, such as <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_architecture" title="Federal architecture">Federal</a>, and the two most recent (<a href="/wiki/Morse_College" title="Morse College">Morse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles_College" title="Ezra Stiles College">Ezra Stiles</a>) have modernist concrete exteriors.</p> 
     <p>Students are assigned to a residential college in their freshman year. Only two residential colleges (<a href="/wiki/Silliman_College" title="Silliman College">Silliman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_College" title="Timothy Dwight College">Timothy Dwight</a>) house freshmen. The majority of on-campus freshmen live on the &quot;<a href="/wiki/Old_Campus" title="Old Campus">Old Campus</a>&quot;, an extensive quadrangle formed by older buildings. Each residential college has its own dining hall, but students are permitted to eat in any residential college dining hall or the large dining facility called &quot;Commons&quot;.</p> 
     <p>Residential colleges are named for important figures or places in university history or notable alumni.</p> 
     <div align="center"> 
      <ul class="gallery"> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"> 
        <div style="width: 155px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"> 
          <div style="margin:35px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_College_(South)_at_Yale.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Berkeley_College_%28South%29_at_Yale.jpg/120px-Berkeley_College_%28South%29_at_Yale.jpg" width="120" height="80" /></a>
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p>Berkeley College</p> 
         </div> 
        </div> </li> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"> 
        <div style="width: 155px"> 
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"> 
          <div style="margin:35px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Branford_In_The_Winter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Branford_In_The_Winter.jpg/120px-Branford_In_The_Winter.jpg" width="120" height="80" /></a>
          </div> 
         </div>

          <p>Branford College</p> 
         </div> 
        </div> </li> 
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_residential_colleges">List of residential colleges</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: List of residential colleges">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>This is a list of residential colleges at Yale.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ol> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Berkeley_College_(Yale)" title="Berkeley College (Yale)">Berkeley College</a>, named for the Rt. Rev. <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a> (1685?1753), early benefactor of Yale.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Branford_College" title="Branford College">Branford College</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Branford,_Connecticut" title="Branford, Connecticut">Branford, Connecticut</a>, where Yale was briefly located.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Calhoun_College" title="Calhoun College">Calhoun College</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, vice-president and influential member of Congress of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Davenport_College" title="Davenport College">Davenport College</a>, named for Rev. <a href="/wiki/John_Davenport_(clergyman)" title="John Davenport (clergyman)" class="mw-redirect">John Davenport</a>, the founder of New Haven. Often called &quot;D'port&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles_College" title="Ezra Stiles College">Ezra Stiles College</a>, named for the Rev. <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Stiles" title="Ezra Stiles">Ezra Stiles</a>, a president of Yale. Generally called &quot;Stiles,&quot; despite an early-1990s crusade by then-master <a href="/wiki/Traugott_Lawler" title="Traugott Lawler">Traugott Lawler</a> to preserve the use of the full name in everyday speech. Its buildings were designed by <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Edwards_College" title="Jonathan Edwards College">Jonathan Edwards College</a>, named for theologian, Yale alumnus, and Princeton co-founder <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Edwards_(theology)" title="Jonathan Edwards (theology)" class="mw-redirect">Jonathan Edwards</a>. Generally called &quot;J.E.&quot; The oldest of the residential colleges, J.E. is the only college with an independent endowment, the Jonathan Edwards Trust.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Morse_College" title="Morse College">Morse College</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Samuel_F._B._Morse" title="Samuel F. B. Morse" class="mw-redirect">Samuel F. B. Morse</a>, inventor of <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph" class="mw-redirect">telegraph</a>. Also designed by <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Pierson_College" title="Pierson College">Pierson College</a>, named for Yale's first rector, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pierson" title="Abraham Pierson">Abraham Pierson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> A statue of <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pierson" title="Abraham Pierson">Abraham Pierson</a> stands on Yale's Old Campus.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Saybrook_College" title="Saybrook College">Saybrook College</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Old_Saybrook,_Connecticut" title="Old Saybrook, Connecticut">Old Saybrook, Connecticut</a>, the town in which Yale was founded.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Silliman_College" title="Silliman College">Silliman College</a>, named for noted scientist and Yale professor <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Silliman" title="Benjamin Silliman">Benjamin Silliman</a>. About half of its structures were originally part of the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Scientific_School" title="Sheffield Scientific School">Sheffield Scientific School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_College" title="Timothy Dwight College">Timothy Dwight College</a>, named for the two Yale presidents of that name, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_IV" title="Timothy Dwight IV">Timothy Dwight IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Dwight_V" title="Timothy Dwight V">Timothy Dwight V</a>. Often abbreviated &quot;T.D.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Trumbull_College" title="Trumbull College">Trumbull College</a>, named for <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Trumbull" title="Jonathan Trumbull">Jonathan Trumbull</a>, first Governor of Connecticut.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
     </ol> 
     <p><br /> In 1998, Yale launched a series of extensive renovations to the older residential buildings, which in many decades of existence had seen only routine maintenance and incremental improvements to plumbing, heating, and electrical and network wiring. Many of these renovations have now been completed, and among other improvements, renovated colleges feature newly built basement facilities including snack bars called &quot;butteries,&quot; game rooms, theaters, athletic facilities, fine arts studios, and music practice rooms.</p> 
     <p>In June 2008, President Levin announced that the Yale Corporation had authorized the construction of two new residential colleges, scheduled to open in 2013. The additional colleges, to be built in the northern part of the campus, will allow for expanded admission and a reduction of crowding in the existing residential colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> Designs have been released, and some public controversy has surfaced over Yale's decision to demolish a number of historic buildings on the site, including a recently constructed library, in order to clear it for the $600&nbsp;million new structures.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_organizations">Student organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Student organizations">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Yale_University_student_organizations" title="List of Yale University student organizations">List of Yale University student organizations</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The university hosts a variety of student journals, magazines, and newspapers. The latter categories include the <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Daily_News" title="Yale Daily News">Yale Daily News</a></i>, which was first published in 1878, the weekly <i><a href="/wiki/Yale_Herald" title="Yale Herald" class="mw-redirect">Yale Herald</a></i>, published since 1986, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yale_Record" title="The Yale Record">The Yale Record</a></i>, which was established in 1872 and is America's oldest college humor magazine. Dwight Hall, an independent, non-profit community service organization, oversees more than 2,000 Yale undergraduates working on more than 70 community service initiatives in New Haven. The Yale College Council runs several agencies that oversee campus wide activities and student services. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_Dramatic_Association" title="Yale Dramatic Association">Yale Dramatic Association</a> and Bulldog Productions cater to the theater and film communities, respectively. In addition, the Yale Drama Coalition<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> serves to coordinate between and provide resources for the various Sudler Fund sponsored theater productions which run each weekend. WYBC Yale Radio<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> is the campus's radio station, owned and operated by students. While students used to broadcast on AM &amp; FM frequencies, they now have an Internet-only stream.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/The_Yale_Political_Union" title="The Yale Political Union" class="mw-redirect">The Yale Political Union</a> is advised by alumni political leaders such as <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_International_Relations_Association" title="Yale International Relations Association">Yale International Relations Association</a> functions as the umbrella organization for the top-ranked Model UN team.</p> 
     <p>The campus also includes several <a href="/wiki/Fraternities_and_sororities" title="Fraternities and sororities" class="mw-redirect">fraternities and sororities</a>. The campus features at least 18 <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a&nbsp;cappella</a> groups, the most famous of which is <a href="/wiki/The_Whiffenpoofs" title="The Whiffenpoofs">The Whiffenpoofs</a>, who are unusual among college singing groups in being made up solely of senior men.</p> 
     <p>Yale's <a href="/wiki/Secret_society" title="Secret society">secret societies</a> include <a href="/wiki/Skull_and_Bones" title="Skull and Bones">Skull and Bones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scroll_and_Key" title="Scroll and Key">Scroll and Key</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wolf%27s_Head_(secret_society)" title="Wolf's Head (secret society)">Wolf's Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_and_Snake" title="Book and Snake">Book and Snake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elihu_(secret_society)" title="Elihu (secret society)">Elihu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berzelius_(secret_society)" title="Berzelius (secret society)">Berzelius</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo_(secret_society)" title="St. Elmo (secret society)">St.&nbsp;Elmo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manuscript_Society" title="Manuscript Society">Manuscript</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mace_and_Chain" title="Mace and Chain">Mace and Chain</a>. The two oldest existing honor societies are the <a href="/wiki/Aurelian_Honor_Society" title="Aurelian Honor Society">Aurelian</a> (1910) and the Torch Honor Society (1916).<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Club" title="Elizabethan Club">Elizabethan Club</a>, a social club, has a membership of undergraduates, graduates, faculty and staff with literary or artistic interests. Membership is by invitation. Members and their guests may enter the &quot;Lizzie's&quot; premises for conversation and tea. The club owns first editions of a Shakespeare Folio, several Shakespeare Quartos, a first edition of Milton's <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i>, among other important literary texts.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions">Traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Traditions">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Yale seniors at graduation smash clay pipes underfoot to symbolize passage from their &quot;<a href="/wiki/Bright_College_Years" title="Bright College Years">bright college years</a>&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> (&quot;Bright College Years,&quot; the University's alma mater, was penned in 1881 by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Strong_Durand" title="Henry Strong Durand">Henry Durand</a>, Class of 1881, to the tune of <i><a href="/wiki/Die_Wacht_am_Rhein" title="Die Wacht am Rhein">Die Wacht am Rhein</a></i>.) Yale's student tour guides tell visitors that students consider it good luck to rub the toe of the statue of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Dwight_Woolsey" title="Theodore Dwight Woolsey">Theodore Dwight Woolsey</a> on Old Campus. Actual students rarely do so.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> In the second half of the twentieth century <a href="/wiki/Bladderball" title="Bladderball">Bladderball</a>, a campus-wide game played with a large inflatable ball, became a popular tradition but was banned by administration due to safety concerns. In spite of administration opposition, students revived the game in 2009 and 2011, but its future remains uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg/220px-Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg/330px-Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg/440px-Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Camp_Gate_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Walter_Camp" title="Walter Camp">Walter Camp</a> Gate at the Yale Athletic Complex.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bulldogs" title="Yale Bulldogs">Yale Bulldogs</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Yale supports 35 varsity athletic teams that compete in the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> Conference, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_College_Athletic_Conference" title="Eastern College Athletic Conference">Eastern College Athletic Conference</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Intercollegiate_Sailing_Association" title="New England Intercollegiate Sailing Association">New England Intercollegiate Sailing Association</a>. Yale athletic teams compete intercollegiately at the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">NCAA</a> Division&nbsp;I level. Like other members of the Ivy League, Yale does not offer athletic scholarships.</p> 
     <p>Yale has numerous athletic facilities, including the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Bowl" title="Yale Bowl">Yale Bowl</a> (the nation's first natural &quot;bowl&quot; stadium, and prototype for such stadiums as the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Memorial_Coliseum" title="Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum">Los Angeles Memorial Coliseum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_(stadium)" title="Rose Bowl (stadium)">Rose Bowl</a>), located at The <a href="/wiki/Walter_Camp" title="Walter Camp">Walter Camp</a> Field athletic complex, and the <a href="/wiki/Payne_Whitney_Gymnasium" title="Payne Whitney Gymnasium">Payne Whitney Gymnasium</a>, the second-largest indoor athletic complex in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> October 21, 2000, marked the dedication of Yale's fourth new boathouse in 157&nbsp;years of collegiate rowing. The <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gilder" title="Richard Gilder">Richard Gilder</a> <a href="/wiki/Boathouse" title="Boathouse">Boathouse</a> is named to honor former Olympic rower Virginia Gilder&nbsp;'79 and her father Richard Gilder&nbsp;'54, who gave $4&nbsp;million towards the $7.5&nbsp;million project. Yale also maintains the <a href="/wiki/Gales_Ferry" title="Gales Ferry" class="mw-redirect">Gales Ferry</a> site where the heavyweight men's team trains for the <a href="/wiki/Yale-Harvard_Boat_Race" title="Yale-Harvard Boat Race" class="mw-redirect">Yale-Harvard Boat Race</a>.</p> 
     <p>Yale crew is the oldest collegiate athletic team in America, and won <a href="/wiki/Olympics" title="Olympics" class="mw-redirect">Olympic Games</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold_Medal" title="Gold Medal" class="mw-redirect">Gold Medal</a> for men's eights in 1924 and 1956. The <a href="/wiki/Yale_Corinthian_Yacht_Club" title="Yale Corinthian Yacht Club">Yale Corinthian Yacht Club</a>, founded in 1881, is the oldest collegiate <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a> club in the world.</p> 
     <p>In 1896, Yale and <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a> played the first known <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> game in the United States. Since 2006, the school's ice hockey clubs have played a commemorative game.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>For kicks, between 1954 and 1982, residential college teams and student organizations played <a href="/wiki/Bladderball" title="Bladderball">bladderball</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale students claim to have invented <a href="/wiki/Frisbee" title="Frisbee" class="mw-redirect">Frisbee</a>, by tossing empty <a href="/wiki/Frisbie_Pie_Company" title="Frisbie Pie Company">Frisbie Pie Company</a> tins.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Song">Song</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Song">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Notable among the songs commonly played and sung at events such as <a href="/wiki/Graduation" title="Graduation">commencement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Convocation" title="Convocation">convocation</a>, alumni gatherings, and athletic games are the alma mater, &quot;<a href="/wiki/Bright_College_Years" title="Bright College Years">Bright College Years</a>&quot;, and the Yale <a href="/wiki/Fight_song" title="Fight song">fight song</a>, &quot;Down the Field.&quot;</p> 
     <p>Two other fight songs, &quot;Bulldog, Bulldog&quot; and &quot;Bingo Eli Yale&quot;, written by <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a> during his undergraduate days, are still sung at football games. Another fight song sung at games is &quot;<a href="/wiki/Boola_Boola" title="Boola Boola">Boola Boola</a>&quot;. According to ?College Fight Songs: An Annotated Anthology? published in 1998, ?Down the Field? ranks as the fourth-greatest fight song of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mascot">Mascot</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Mascot">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The school mascot is &quot;<a href="/wiki/Handsome_Dan" title="Handsome Dan">Handsome Dan</a>&quot;, the known Yale <a href="/wiki/Bulldog" title="Bulldog">bulldog</a>, and the Yale <a href="/wiki/Fight_song" title="Fight song">fight song</a> (written by <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a> while he was a student at Yale) contains the <a href="/wiki/Refrain" title="Refrain">refrain</a>, &quot;Bulldog, bulldog, bow wow wow.&quot; The school color, since 1894, is <a href="/wiki/Yale_Blue" title="Yale Blue">Yale Blue</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Yale's <a href="/wiki/Handsome_Dan" title="Handsome Dan">Handsome Dan</a> is believed to be the first college <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a> in America, having been established in 1889.<sup id="cite_ref-YaleBulldogs_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YaleBulldogs-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Yale athletics are supported by the <a href="/wiki/Yale_Precision_Marching_Band" title="Yale Precision Marching Band">Yale Precision Marching Band</a>. &quot;Precision&quot; is used here ironically; the band is a scatter-style band that runs wildly between formations rather than actually marching.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> The band attends every home football game and many away, as well as most hockey and basketball games throughout the winter.</p> 
     <p>Yale intramural sports are also a significant aspect of student life. Students compete for their respective residential colleges, fostering a friendly rivalry. The year is divided into fall, winter, and spring seasons, each of which includes about ten different sports. About half the sports are coeducational. At the end of the year, the residential college with the most points (not all sports count equally) wins the Tyng Cup.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people">Notable people</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Notable people">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benefactors">Benefactors</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Benefactors">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Yale has had many financial supporters, but some stand out by the magnitude or timeliness of their contributions. Among those who have made large donations commemorated at the university are: <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Yale" title="Elihu Yale">Elihu Yale</a>; <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Dummer" title="Jeremiah Dummer">Jeremiah Dummer</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Harkness" title="Edward S. Harkness" class="mw-redirect">Harkness family</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Harkness" title="Edward S. Harkness" class="mw-redirect">Edward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Harkness" title="Anna Harkness" class="mw-redirect">Anna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Harkness" title="William Harkness">William</a>); the <a href="/wiki/Beinecke_Rare_Book_and_Manuscript_Library" title="Beinecke Rare Book and Manuscript Library">Beinecke</a> family (Edwin, Frederick, and Walter); <a href="/wiki/John_William_Sterling" title="John William Sterling">John William Sterling</a>; <a href="/wiki/Payne_Whitney" title="Payne Whitney">Payne Whitney</a>; <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Sheffield" title="Joseph E. Sheffield" class="mw-redirect">Joseph E. Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Mellon" title="Paul Mellon">Paul Mellon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_B._G._Murphy" title="Charles B. G. Murphy">Charles B.&nbsp;G. Murphy</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_K._Lanman" title="William K. Lanman">William K. Lanman</a>. The Yale Class of 1954, led by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gilder" title="Richard Gilder">Richard Gilder</a>, donated $70&nbsp;million in commemoration of their 50th&nbsp;reunion.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_alumni_and_faculty">Notable alumni and faculty</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Yale_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Notable alumni and faculty">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink">
      Further information: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Yale_University_people" title="List of Yale University people">List of Yale University people</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Yale_Law_School_alumni" title="List of Yale Law School alumni">List of Yale Law School alumni</a>
     </div>

      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">

       <div class="thumbcaption">

         <a href="/wiki/File:William_Howard_Taft,_Bain_bw_photo_portrait,_1908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
